Immune Globulin

Class: Antitoxins and Immune Globulins
VA Class: IM500
CAS Number: 9007-83-4
Brands: Asceniv, Bivigam, Carimune, Cutaquig, Cuvitru, Flebogamma, Gammagard, GamaSTAN, Gammaked, Gammaplex, Gamunex-C, Hizentra, Hyqvia, Octagam, Panzyga, Privigen, Xembify

  • Dosage
  • Cautions
  • Interactions

Warning

    Thrombosis

  • Thrombosis may occur with immune globulin preparations.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>294</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>327</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>331</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>340</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>341</sup></body></html> (See Thrombosis under Cautions.)

indwelling central vascular catheters, hyperviscosity, and other cardiovascular risk factors.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>     Renal Dysfunction and Acute Renal Failure

  • Renal dysfunction, acute renal failure, osmotic nephrosis, and death may occur with immune globulin preparations.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html> (See Renal Effects under Cautions.)

  • Patients predisposed or at increased risk of renal dysfunction include those with any degree of preexisting renal insufficiency, diabetes mellitus, age >65 years, volume depletion, sepsis, paraproteinemia, or concomitant therapy with nephrotoxic drugs.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html>

  • Renal dysfunction and acute renal failure occur more commonly in patients receiving IGIV preparations containing sucrose.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html>

  • In patients at risk of renal dysfunction or failure, administer immune globulin using minimum dose and minimum infusion rate practicable.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html>

Introduction

Immune globulin IM (IGIM), immune globulin IV (IGIV), and immune globulin subcutaneous; sterile, nonpyrogenic preparations of globulins containing many antibodies normally present in adult human blood.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>294</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>327</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>331</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html>

Uses for Immune Globulin

Hepatitis A Virus (HAV) Infection (Preexposure Prophylaxis)

IGIM (GamaSTAN) is used to provide short-term passive immunity to HAV infection for preexposure prophylaxis in certain susceptible individuals at risk of exposure to the virus.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>231</sup></body></html>

Primary immunization with an age-appropriate schedule of hepatitis A vaccine is preferred for HAV preexposure prophylaxis in most adults, adolescents, children, and infants ≥12 months of age, unless contraindicated, since active immunization provides long-term protection.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

IGIM can be used alone for HAV preexposure prophylaxis when the vaccine is unavailable or cannot be used (e.g., infants <12 months of age, individuals hypersensitive to vaccine components).<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Combined passive immunization with IGIM and active immunization with hepatitis A vaccine should be used in those who require both immediate and long-term protection against HAV.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Travelers to areas with intermediate or high levels of endemic HAV are at substantial risk of acquiring the disease.<html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> Risk is highest for those who live in or visit rural areas, trek in back country areas, or frequently eat or drink in settings with poor sanitation.<html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> However, cases of travel-related HAV can occur in travelers who have standard tourist itineraries and accommodations and food consumption behaviors considered low risk.<html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> Consult CDC website ([Web]) for information regarding which countries have intermediate or high levels of HAV endemicity.<html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html>

USPHS Advisory Committee on Immunization Practices (ACIP) and CDC recommend HAV preexposure prophylaxis in all susceptible individuals (i.e., unvaccinated, partially vaccinated, or never infected) traveling for any purpose, frequency, or duration to areas where HAV endemicity is intermediate or high.<html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> In addition, because of the complexity of determining HAV endemicity globally, some experts advise individuals traveling outside the US to consider HAV preexposure prophylaxis regardless of travel destination.<html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Hepatitis A vaccine is preferred for HAV preexposure prophylaxis in most travelers, unless contraindicated, and should be administered as soon as travel is considered.<html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> A single dose of hepatitis A vaccine administered before departure can provide adequate protection for most healthy travelers.<html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> For optimal protection in travelers at greatest risk for HAV (adults >40 years of age or individuals with altered immunocompetence, chronic liver disease or other chronic medical condition) who plan to depart in <2 weeks, a single dose of IGIM can be given concurrently with the initial dose of hepatitis A vaccine (at a different site).<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Hepatitis A Virus (HAV) Infection (Postexposure Prophylaxis)

IGIM (GamaSTAN) is used to provide short-term passive immunity for postexposure prophylaxis of HAV in susceptible individuals with recent (within 2 weeks) exposure to the virus.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Although IGIM is 80–90% effective in preventing symptomatic HAV infection if administered within 2 weeks of exposure,<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> monovalent hepatitis A vaccine appears to be as effective as IGIM for HAV postexposure prophylaxis in healthy individuals 1 through 40 years of age if administered within 2 weeks of exposure.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>287</sup></body></html> The vaccine offers certain advantages over IGIM (e.g., induces active immunity and longer protection, more readily available, easier to administer, greater patient acceptance).<html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> However, if only IGIM or only hepatitis A vaccine is available at the time HAV postexposure prophylaxis is needed, either product can be administered, unless contraindicated.<html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Combined passive immunization with IGIM and active immunization with hepatitis A vaccine is recommended for postexposure prophylaxis of HAV in susceptible individuals ≥12 months of age who are immunocompromised or have chronic liver disease and may be considered in healthy adults >40 years of age since such individuals may have lower seroconversion rates after vaccination and are at increased risk of more severe manifestations of HAV.<html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

HAV postexposure prophylaxis is not necessary in healthy individuals who have previously received hepatitis A vaccine according to the age-appropriate immunization schedule.<html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

If HAV postexposure prophylaxis is indicated, administer hepatitis A vaccine and/or IGIM as appropriate as soon as possible (within 2 weeks of exposure).<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> Data not available regarding efficacy of hepatitis A vaccine or IGIM administered for HAV postexposure prophylaxis >2 weeks after exposure.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html> Routine serologic screening of contacts for markers of HAV infection prior to administration of HAV postexposure prophylaxis is not recommended since this would delay prophylaxis.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

In individuals in whom IGIM is preferred for HAV postexposure prophylaxis, give a dose of hepatitis A vaccine concurrently (using different syringes and different injection sites) if the vaccine is indicated for other reasons (e.g., catch-up vaccination, preexposure vaccination in high-risk groups) and is not contraindicated.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html>

Infants <12 months of age and whenever hepatitis A vaccine is contraindicated: IGIM recommended for HAV postexposure prophylaxis.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Healthy individuals 12 months through 40 years of age: Monovalent hepatitis A vaccine recommended for HAV postexposure prophylaxis.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Healthy adults >40 years of age: Monovalent hepatitis A vaccine recommended for HAV postexposure prophylaxis.<html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> Also consider concomitant use of IGIM (administered at a separate site) based on risk assessment.<html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Individuals ≥12 months of age who are immunocompromised or have chronic liver disease: Combined regimen of monovalent hepatitis A vaccine and IGIM (at separate sites) recommended for HAV postexposure prophylaxis.<html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Travelers not adequately immunized with hepatitis A vaccine exposed to HAV (within the past 2 weeks) should receive HAV postexposure prophylaxis.<html><head></head><body><sup>115</sup></body></html>

Individuals not adequately immunized with hepatitis A vaccine who are household or sexual (heterosexual or homosexual) contacts (within the past 2 weeks) of an individual with serologically confirmed HAV should receive HAV postexposure prophylaxis.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Child-care center staff and attendees not adequately immunized with hepatitis A vaccine should receive HAV postexposure prophylaxis if ≥1 case of HAV is recognized in attendees or if HAV is recognized in ≥2 households of center attendees (within the past 2 weeks).<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> If ≥1 case of HAV occurs among employees of a child-care center, consider HAV postexposure prophylaxis for other staff and for attendees based on duties, hygienic practices, and presence of symptoms in the index case while at work.<html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> In centers that do not provide care to children who wear diapers, HAV postexposure prophylaxis is indicated only in classroom contacts of the index patient.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

School staff and attendees not adequately immunized with hepatitis A vaccine may receive HAV postexposure prophylaxis if transmission within the school setting is documented.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> Schoolroom exposure generally does not pose an appreciable risk of infection and HAV postexposure prophylaxis is not indicated if only a single case occurs and the source of infection is outside the school.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> However, HAV postexposure prophylaxis is recommended for susceptible close personal contacts of the index case if an epidemiologic investigation indicates that HAV transmission has occurred (e.g., among students in a school).<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Individuals not adequately immunized with hepatitis A vaccine who are exposed to an infected food handler should receive HAV postexposure prophylaxis (within 2 weeks) if they are food handlers at the same establishment.<html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> Because common-source transmission to patrons is unlikely, HAV postexposure prophylaxis is not usually indicated for patrons, but may be considered if the food handler directly handled uncooked or cooked food and had diarrhea or poor hygienic practices and if patrons can be identified and given prophylaxis within 2 weeks after exposure.<html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> Settings where repeated HAV exposure might have occurred (e.g., institutional cafeterias) warrant stronger consideration of postexposure prophylaxis for patrons.<html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Healthcare personnel are not at substantially increased risk for HAV infection as the result of occupational exposures and healthcare-associated HAV transmission is rare.<html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>235</sup></body></html> HAV postexposure prophylaxis within the healthcare setting should be considered on a case-by-case basis if the risk for HAV exposure is considered high.<html><head></head><body><sup>186</sup></body></html>

Neonates of HAV-infected mothers do not usually need HAV postexposure prophylaxis since perinatal transmission of HAV is rare.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> Although efficacy not established, some experts suggest that the infant† receive HAV postexposure prophylaxis with IGIM if the mother's symptoms began between 2 weeks before and 1 week after delivery.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html>

Measles

IGIM (GamaSTAN) and IGIV† are used to prevent or modify symptoms of measles (rubeola) in susceptible individuals exposed to the disease <6 days previously.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>

Individuals born before 1957 and individuals with documentation of adequate vaccination against measles at ≥12 months of age, laboratory evidence of measles immunity, or laboratory confirmation of prior measles infection have acceptable presumptive evidence of measles immunity.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html> Consider other individuals susceptible to measles.<html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html>

Postexposure vaccination (i.e., within 72 hours of exposure) with a vaccine containing measles virus vaccine live (e.g., measles, mumps, and rubella virus vaccine live; MMR) is recommended by ACIP and AAP and is preferred for postexposure prophylaxis against measles in most susceptible individuals ≥12 months of age who are exposed to measles in most settings (e.g., day-care facilities, schools, colleges, health-care facilities), provided the vaccine can be given within 72 hours of the exposure and is not contraindicated.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html>

Postexposure prophylaxis with immune globulin (i.e., within 6 days of exposure) is recommended in certain individuals at risk for severe disease and complications from measles who cannot receive the vaccine, including infants <12 months of age, pregnant women without evidence of measles immunity, and severely immunocompromised patients.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>

When immune globulin is indicated for measles postexposure prophylaxis, ACIP and AAP recommend IGIM for such prophylaxis in infants <12 months of age and IGIV for such prophylaxis in susceptible pregnant women and severely immunocompromised individuals.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html>

Because infants are at higher risk for severe measles and complications and are susceptible to measles if mothers are nonimmune or have low antibody titers, ACIP and AAP state that infants <12 months of age† should receive IGIM following exposure to measles.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html> Alternatively, infants 6 through 11 months of age can receive postexposure vaccination with MMR, provided the vaccine can be administered within 72 hours of exposure.<html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html>

In severely immunocompromised patients at increased risk for severe measles and complications (e.g., those with severe primary immunodeficiency, bone marrow transplant recipients, patients being treated for acute lymphocytic leukemia, HIV-infected patients with AIDS), ACIP and AAP recommend postexposure prophylaxis with IGIV within 6 days following exposure, regardless of vaccination status.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html>

Because passive immunity to measles following administration of IGIM or IGIV is temporary (unless modified or typical measles occurs), initiate immunization with MMR 6 months after IGIM was given or 8 months after IGIV was given, providing the individual is ≥12 months of age and there are no contraindications to the vaccine.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html>

Do not give MMR concurrently with immune globulin.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>134</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html> (See Specific Drugs and Laboratory Tests under Interactions.)

Do not use immune globulin in an attempt to control measles outbreaks.<html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html>

Mumps

Immune globulin, including IGIM, is not effective for prevention of mumps<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html> and should not be used for prophylaxis or treatment of mumps.<html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>

Poliomyelitis

IGIM is not indicated for and should not be used for prophylaxis or treatment of poliomyelitis.<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>

Rubella

Immune globulin, including IGIM, has not been shown to prevent rubella and should not be used for that purpose.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>133</sup></body></html>

IGIM (GamaSTAN) is labeled by FDA for use to modify symptoms of rubella in pregnant women who will not consider therapeutic abortion;<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html> do not use for routine prophylaxis of rubella in early pregnancy in women who have not been exposed.<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>

Although some studies suggest that use of IGIM in susceptible pregnant women exposed to rubella may lessen the likelihood of rubella infection and associated adverse fetal effects,<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html> ACIP and AAP state do not use IGIM routinely for postexposure prophylaxis of rubella in early pregnancy or any other circumstance.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>131</sup></body></html> These experts state that use of IGIM after rubella exposure will not prevent infection or viremia, but may modify or suppress symptoms and can create an unwarranted sense of security.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>131</sup></body></html> Infants with congenital rubella syndrome have been born to women who received IGIM shortly after exposure to the disease.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>131</sup></body></html>

Varicella

IGIV has been used and is recommended as an alternative to varicella-zoster immune globulin (VZIG) for postexposure prophylaxis of varicella† in susceptible individuals when VZIG is unavailable.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>156</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>268</sup></body></html>

IGIM (GamaSTAN) is labeled by FDA for use to modify symptoms of varicella (chickenpox) in susceptible individuals;<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html> do not use for routine prophylaxis or treatment of varicella.<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>

Although manufacturer states that IGIM may be considered an alternative to VZIG for postexposure prophylaxis of varicella in susceptible individuals who are immunocompromised,<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html> IGIV (not IGIM) is recommended when VZIG is unavailable.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>156</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>269</sup></body></html>

VZIG is the preferred immune globulin for postexposure prophylaxis of varicella in individuals who do not have evidence of immunity (i.e., without a history of varicella or varicella vaccination) and are at high risk for severe disease and complications (e.g., HIV-infected or other immunocompromised individuals, pregnant women).<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>146</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>155</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>156</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>269</sup></body></html>

Clinical data demonstrating effectiveness of IGIV for postexposure prophylaxis of varicella not available.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> Commercially available IGIV preparations contain anti-varicella antibody titers, but the titer of any specific IGIV lot is uncertain since IGIV is not routinely tested for anti-varicella antibodies.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>156</sup></body></html>

ACIP, AAP, CDC, NIH, and others state that HIV-infected adults, adolescents, or children or other individuals who are receiving IGIV replacement therapy (≥400 mg/kg given at regular intervals) and received a dose of IGIV within 3 weeks prior to exposure to wild-type varicella-zoster virus are likely to be protected and probably do not require postexposure prophylaxis with VZIG or IGIV.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>146</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>155</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>156</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>269</sup></body></html>

Although VZIG or IGIV given shortly after exposure to varicella-zoster virus can prevent or modify the course of the disease, immune globulin is not effective once disease is established.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html>

Primary Immunodeficiency Diseases

IGIV (i.e., Asceniv 10%, Bivigam 10%, Carimune NF, Flebogamma 5% DIF, Flebogamma 10% DIF, Gammagard S/D [IgA <1 mcg/mL], Gammagard 10%, Gammaked 10%, Gammaplex 5%, Gammaplex 10%, Gamunex-C 10%, Octagam 5%, Panzyga 10%, Privigen 10%) is used for replacement therapy to promote passive immunity in patients with primary humoral immunodeficiency who are unable to produce sufficient amounts of IgG antibodies.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>274</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>275</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>276</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html> This includes, but is not limited to, patients with common variable immunodeficiency (CVID), X-linked agammaglobulinemia, congenital agammaglobulinemia, Wiskott-Aldrich syndrome, and severe combined immunodeficiencies (SCID).<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>327</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html>

Immune globulin subcutaneous (i.e., Cutaquig 16.5%, Cuvitru 20%, Gammagard Liquid 10%, Gammaked 10%, Gamunex-C 10%, Hizentra 20%, Xembify 20%) and immune globulin subcutaneous in conjunction with recombinant human hyaluronidase (Hyqvia; immune globulin subcutaneous 10% copackaged with recombinant human hyaluronidase) are used for replacement therapy in patients with primary humoral immunodeficiency.<html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>294</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>327</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>331</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>340</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>341</sup></body></html> This includes, but is not limited to, patients with CVID, X-linked agammaglobulinemia, congenital agammaglobulinemia, Wiskott-Aldrich syndrome, and SCID.<html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>294</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>327</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>331</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>340</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>341</sup></body></html>

IGIV and immune globulin subcutaneous are contraindicated in IgA-deficient individuals with antibodies against IgA and a history of hypersensitivity.<html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>294</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>327</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>331</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>340</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>341</sup></body></html> (See Contraindications under Cautions and see IgA Deficiency under Cautions.)

Idiopathic Thrombocytopenic Purpura (ITP)

IGIV (i.e., Carimune NF, Flebogamma 10% DIF, Gammagard S/D [IgA <1 mcg/mL], Gammaked 10%, Gammaplex 5%, Gammaplex 10%, Gamunex-C 10%, Octagam 10%, Panzyga 10%, Privigen 10%) is used in the management of ITP (also known as immune thrombocytopenic purpura or immune thrombocytopenia).<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>138</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>139</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>335</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html> IGIV is designated an orphan drug by FDA for treatment of ITP.<html><head></head><body><sup>148</sup></body></html>

IGIV is used to increase platelet counts<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html> to prevent and/or control bleeding in patients with ITP<html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html> or to allow a patient with ITP to undergo surgery.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html>

Individuals with B-cell Chronic Lymphocytic Leukemia (CLL)

IGIV (i.e., Gammagard S/D [IgA <1 mcg/mL]) is used for prevention of bacterial infections in patients with hypogammaglobulinemia and/or recurrent bacterial infections associated with B-cell CLL.<html><head></head><body><sup>144</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>145</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>153</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>157</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html>

Kawasaki Disease

IGIV (i.e., Gammagard S/D [IgA <1 mcg/mL]) is used in conjunction with aspirin therapy for initial treatment of the acute phase of Kawasaki disease.<html><head></head><body><sup>103</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>104</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>128</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>144</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>145</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>157</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>163</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>240</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>241</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>299</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>300</sup></body></html>

AAP, AHA, and American College of Chest Physicians (ACCP) state that combined therapy with IGIV and aspirin should be administered as soon as possible after Kawasaki disease is diagnosed or strongly suspected (optimally within 7–10 days of disease onset).<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>299</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>300</sup></body></html> In those with a delayed diagnosis (i.e., >10 days after disease onset), AAP and AHA suggest initiation of combined therapy with IGIV and aspirin if the patient has unexplained persistent fever or aneurysms and manifestations of ongoing systemic inflammation (elevated erythrocyte sedimentation rate [ESR] or C-reactive protein [CRP >3 mg/dL]) or evolving CAD.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>299</sup></body></html>

Approximately 10–20% of patients with Kawasaki disease fail to respond to initial treatment with IGIV and aspirin therapy and have persistent fever or recurrent fever after an initial afebrile period.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>299</sup></body></html> In such situations, AHA and AAP state that IGIV retreatment and continued aspirin therapy is a reasonable option.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>299</sup></body></html> Use of additional or alternative anti-inflammatory or immunosuppressive agents may be necessary in IGIV-resistant patients.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>299</sup></body></html>

Coronary artery abnormalities develop in 15–25% of children with Kawasaki disease if they are not treated within 10 days of fever onset;<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>299</sup></body></html> approximately 2–4% of patients develop coronary artery abnormalities despite prompt treatment with IGIV and aspirin therapy.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> Long-term management of those who develop coronary abnormalities depends on the severity of coronary involvement and may include use of low-dose aspirin, anticoagulants, anti-thrombotic agents, and/or antiplatelet agents.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>299</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>300</sup></body></html>

Consult specialized references for additional information on management of Kawasaki disease, including long-term management in individuals with coronary abnormalities.<html><head></head><body><sup>299</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>300</sup></body></html>

Chronic Inflammatory Demyelinating Polyneuropathy (CIDP)

IGIV (i.e., Gammaked 10%, Gamunex-C 10%, Privigen 10%) is used for the treatment of CIDP to improve neuromuscular disability and impairment and for maintenance therapy to prevent relapse.<html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>298</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html>

Immune globulin subcutaneous (i.e., Hizentra 20%) is used for the treatment of CIDP in adults as maintenance therapy to prevent relapse of neuromuscular disability and impairment.<html><head></head><body><sup>343</sup></body></html> Immune globulin subcutaneous is designated an orphan drug by FDA for treatment of CIDP.<html><head></head><body><sup>148</sup></body></html>

Some clinicians consider IGIV the preferred treatment for CIDP, especially in children†, patients with poor venous access that precludes use of plasma exchange, and in those susceptible to complications of long-term corticosteroid therapy.<html><head></head><body><sup>305</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html>

Multifocal Motor Neuropathy

IGIV (i.e., Gammagard Liquid 10%) is used for maintenance treatment to improve muscle strength and disability in adults with multifocal motor neuropathy (MMN);<html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> designated an orphan drug by FDA for this use.<html><head></head><body><sup>148</sup></body></html>

Some clinicians recommend IGIV as a treatment of choice for MMN<html><head></head><body><sup>301</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>305</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>310</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>311</sup></body></html> when disability is severe enough to warrant treatment.<html><head></head><body><sup>311</sup></body></html>

Other Neurologic and Neuromuscular Disorders

IGIV is used in the treatment of Guillain-Barré syndrome† (GBS);<html><head></head><body><sup>165</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>219</sup></body></html> IGIV has been used in the management of multiple sclerosis† (MS).<html><head></head><body><sup>301</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>305</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>310</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>318</sup></body></html> Benefits (e.g., reduced exacerbations, reduced disability scores) reported in some patients with relapsing-remitting MS,<html><head></head><body><sup>301</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>305</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>310</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>318</sup></body></html> but these findings not confirmed with subsequent studies.<html><head></head><body><sup>333</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>334</sup></body></html> Although some clinicians suggest that IGIV can be considered as a potentially effective second- or third-line treatment in patients with relapsing-remitting MS,<html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>310</sup></body></html> others state IGIV not recommended for treatment of relapsing-remitting<html><head></head><body><sup>333</sup></body></html> or secondary progressive MS<html><head></head><body><sup>310</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>333</sup></body></html> or treatment of chronic symptoms of MS.<html><head></head><body><sup>310</sup></body></html>

IGIV may provide some benefits in the management of stiff person syndrome† (Moersch-Woltmann syndrome);<html><head></head><body><sup>301</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>304</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>305</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>310</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>312</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>321</sup></body></html> designated an orphan drug by FDA for this use.<html><head></head><body><sup>148</sup></body></html> Although efficacy and safety not established, some clinicians recommend use of IGIV as second-line treatment when other treatments have been unsuccessful or cannot be used.<html><head></head><body><sup>301</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>310</sup></body></html>

IGIV has been used in a limited number of children with intractable epilepsy†.<html><head></head><body><sup>218</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> There is some evidence that IGIV may be beneficial in some patients with Lennox-Gastaut syndrome† or Rasmussen syndrome†,<html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>310</sup></body></html> but further study needed.<html><head></head><body><sup>145</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>157</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>165</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>218</sup></body></html> Although efficacy and safety not established, some clinicians suggest IGIV can be considered in children with intractable epilepsy if they have not responded to antiepileptic agents and corticosteroids,<html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>310</sup></body></html> especially if they are otherwise candidates for surgical resection.<html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html>

Infections in HIV-infected Individuals

IGIV has been used in an attempt to control or prevent infections and improve immunologic parameters in children with symptomatic HIV infection† who are immunosuppressed in association with AIDS or AIDS-related complex (ARC).<html><head></head><body><sup>130</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>139</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>144</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>145</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>156</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>157</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>175</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>176</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>177</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>178</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>179</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>180</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>181</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>184</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>216</sup></body></html>

IGIV also has been used in an attempt to control or prevent infections in HIV-infected adults†.<html><head></head><body><sup>130</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>144</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>145</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>157</sup></body></html>

IGIV reduces incidence of recurrent bacterial infections and sepsis, including upper respiratory tract infections, in adults and children with symptomatic HIV infection.<html><head></head><body><sup>144</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>145</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>157</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>175</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>176</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>177</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>178</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>216</sup></body></html>

AAP, CDC, NIH, and other experts state that HIV-infected children with hypogammaglobulinemia (IgG <400 mg/dL) should receive primary prophylaxis with IGIV (400 mg/kg once every 2–4 weeks) to prevent serious bacterial infections (e.g., those caused by Streptococcus pneumoniae or other invasive bacteria).<html><head></head><body><sup>156</sup></body></html> These experts also recommend IGIV as an alternative to co-trimoxazole for secondary prophylaxis of serious bacterial infections in certain HIV-infected children.<html><head></head><body><sup>156</sup></body></html>

Infections in Bone Marrow Transplant (BMT) Recipients

IGIV has been used in adults and children undergoing BMT† to decrease the risk of infections (e.g., septicemia), interstitial pneumonia of infectious or idiopathic etiologies, and acute graft-versus-host disease (GVHD).<html><head></head><body><sup>221</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>223</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>224</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>225</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html>

Effect of IGIV on the incidence of cytomegalovirus (CMV) infection, other infections, or GVHD in patients undergoing allogeneic BMT is unclear.<html><head></head><body><sup>221</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>222</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>223</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>224</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>225</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>304</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> IGIV prophylaxis in BMT patients does not appear to affect survival or risk of cancer relapse, and the long-term effects of such therapy remain to be determined.<html><head></head><body><sup>221</sup></body></html>

Although efficacy and safety in BMT patients not established, some clinicians suggest that IGIV be used for prophylaxis in all allogeneic BMT patients, especially CMV-positive patients or those who have received a transplant from a CMV-positive donor.<html><head></head><body><sup>222</sup></body></html>

Some clinicians suggest that, although there is a perceived benefit of IGIV prophylaxis in infants with severe combined immunodeficiency or other primary immunodeficiency diseases undergoing BMT, the effect of IGIV in these children is difficult to study since they generally are receiving IGIV for replacement therapy.<html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> These clinicians also state that use of IGIV appears to offer little benefit in patients with malignancies undergoing HLA-identical sibling BMT and that additional study is needed to determine whether the drug is beneficial in those undergoing HLA-matched unrelated BMT or cord blood transplants.<html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html>

Infections in Hematopoietic Stem Cell Transplant (HSCT) Recipients

CDC, IDSA, and American Society of Blood and Marrow Transplantation (ASBMT) state that, although routine use of IGIV for prophylaxis is not recommended for autologous HSCT recipients, some clinicians recommend use of IGIV to prevent bacterial infections (e.g., S. pneumoniae sinopulmonary infections) in adult, adolescent, or pediatric allogeneic HSCT recipients† who experience severe hypogammaglobulinemia (IgG <400 mg/dL) within the first 100 days after transplant.<html><head></head><body><sup>262</sup></body></html>

Routine administration of IGIV in HSCT recipients >90 days after HSCT is not recommended in the absence of severe hypogammaglobulinemia.<html><head></head><body><sup>262</sup></body></html>

Infections in Low-birthweight Neonates

IGIV has been used for prophylaxis and treatment of infections in certain high-risk, preterm, low-birthweight neonates†.<html><head></head><body><sup>130</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>137</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>144</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>145</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>157</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>162</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>167</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>168</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>169</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>170</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>171</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>172</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>214</sup></body></html> However, use of IGIV for prophylaxis of infections in high-risk neonates is controversial.<html><head></head><body><sup>144</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>145</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>157</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>167</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>170</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>183</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>214</sup></body></html> AAP does not recommend routine use of IGIV for prophylaxis of infections in preterm neonates.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html>

Autoimmune Neutropenia and Autoimmune Hemolytic Anemia

IGIV has been used with some success in a limited number of adults and children for the treatment of autoimmune neutropenia†.<html><head></head><body><sup>130</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>138</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>140</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>145</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>157</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>164</sup></body></html> May be beneficial in some patients,<html><head></head><body><sup>304</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> but unclear whether IGIV offers any advantage over corticosteroid therapy.<html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html>

IGIV has been used with variable results in patients with autoimmune hemolytic anemia†.<html><head></head><body><sup>130</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>140</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>142</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>143</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>157</sup></body></html> Some clinicians state IGIV should be used in the management of autoimmune hemolytic anemia only in those who fail to respond to other treatment options.<html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html>

Coronavirus Disease 2019 (COVID-19)

IGIV is being investigated for and has been used in the treatment of COVID-19† caused by severe acute respiratory syndrome coronavirus 2 (SARS-CoV-2).<html><head></head><body><sup>346</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>347</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>348</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>349</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>350</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>352</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>354</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>355</sup></body></html>

There is some evidence that commercially available IGIV may contain antibodies against some previously circulating coronaviruses,<html><head></head><body><sup>344</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>351</sup></body></html> including antibodies that cross-react with SARS-CoV-2 antigens.<html><head></head><body><sup>351</sup></body></html> In addition, it has been suggested that the various immunomodulatory and anti-inflammatory effects of IGIV potentially could help combat the hyperinflammatory state and symptoms of cytokine release syndrome in patients with severe COVID-19.<html><head></head><body><sup>346</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>351</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>353</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>354</sup></body></html> However, it is unclear whether IGIV contains clinically important titers of SARS-CoV-2 antibodies and additional study is needed to determine whether the general immunomodulatory effects of IGIV provide benefits in patients with COVID-19.<html><head></head><body><sup>354</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>356</sup></body></html>

Specific SARS-CoV-2 immune globulin prepared using plasma obtained from individuals who have recovered from COVID-19 (not commercially available in US) also is being investigated for treatment of COVID-19.<html><head></head><body><sup>355</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>356</sup></body></html> Such concentrated immune globulin (hyperimmune globulin) preparations containing antibodies specific to SARS-CoV-2 could potentially suppress the virus and modify the inflammatory response to COVID-19 infection.<html><head></head><body><sup>356</sup></body></html>

NIH COVID-19 Treatment Guidelines Panel recommends against use of commercially available IGIV for treatment of COVID-19, except in the context of a clinical trial; this does not preclude use of IGIV when it is otherwise indicated for treatment of complications arising during the course of COVID-19 disease.<html><head></head><body><sup>356</sup></body></html> NIH panel states that it is not known whether products derived from plasma of donors without confirmation of prior SARS-CoV-2 infection contain high titers of SARS-CoV-2 neutralizing antibodies and, although other blood components in IGIV may have general immunomodulatory effects, it is unclear whether these theoretical effects benefit patients with COVID-19.<html><head></head><body><sup>356</sup></body></html>

Surviving Sepsis Campaign COVID-19 subcommittee (joint initiative of Society of Critical Care Medicine and European Society of Intensive Care Medicine) suggests against routine use of IGIV in critically ill adults with COVID-19 because efficacy data are not available, commercially available IGIV preparations unlikely to contain adequate titers of neutralizing antibodies against SARS-CoV-2, and IGIV can be associated with increased risk of severe adverse effects (e.g., anaphylaxis, aseptic meningitis, renal failure, thromboembolism, hemolytic reactions, transfusion-related lung injury).<html><head></head><body><sup>357</sup></body></html>

NIH COVID-19 panel states that there are insufficient data to date to recommend either for or against use of investigational SARS-CoV-2 immune globulin for treatment of COVID-19.<html><head></head><body><sup>356</sup></body></html>

Dermatomyositis and Polymyositis

IGIV has been used in the treatment of dermatomyositis† and polymyositis†.<html><head></head><body><sup>220</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>301</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>304</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>305</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>310</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>312</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>318</sup></body></html> IGIV and immune globulin subcutaneous designated as orphan drugs for treatment of dermatomyositis.<html><head></head><body><sup>148</sup></body></html>

IGIV has resulted in improvements (e.g., in muscle strength, neuromuscular symptoms, rash, scaling) in a limited number of patients with biopsy-proven, treatment-resistant dermatomyositis.<html><head></head><body><sup>220</sup></body></html> Although efficacy and safety not established, it has been suggested that IGIV (usually in conjunction with corticosteroids) may be beneficial as second-line therapy in patients with dermatomyositis when other therapies are unsuccessful or cannot be used.<html><head></head><body><sup>218</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>301</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>305</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>310</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>312</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>318</sup></body></html>

Graves’ Ophthalmopathy

IGIV has been used with some success in the management of Graves’ ophthalmopathy†.<html><head></head><body><sup>301</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>302</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>303</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>304</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>305</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html>

Some patients responded to IGIV with improvements in diplopia, proptosis, visual acuity, and intraocular pressure;<html><head></head><body><sup>302</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>303</sup></body></html> response rate appeared similar to that obtained with corticosteroid treatment.<html><head></head><body><sup>301</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>302</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>303</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>305</sup></body></html>

Systemic Lupus Erythematosus

IGIV has been used with some success in the treatment of systemic lupus erythematosus† (SLE);<html><head></head><body><sup>218</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>301</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>304</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>305</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> efficacy and safety not definitely established and additional study needed.<html><head></head><body><sup>305</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html>

Some clinicians suggest use of IGIV may be considered in patients with severe active SLE when other drugs have been ineffective or not tolerated;<html><head></head><body><sup>218</sup></body></html> other clinicians recommend caution.<html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html>

Tetanus

IGIV has been recommended as an alternative for the treatment of tetanus† when tetanus immune globulin (TIG) is unavailable;<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> TIG is the immune globulin of choice.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html>

IGIV has been recommended as an alternative for postexposure prophylaxis of tetanus† in individuals with tetanus-prone wounds when TIG is unavailable;<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> TIG is the immune globulin of choice.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html>

Toxic Shock Syndrome

IGIV has been used as an adjunct to anti-infectives and surgical intervention in the treatment of staphylococcal or streptococcal toxic shock syndrome† or necrotizing fasciitis† in severely ill patients.<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>201</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>306</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>323</sup></body></html>

Although data are limited and efficacy and safety not established, AAP and others suggest use of IGIV may be considered as an adjunct in the management of severe staphylococcal or streptococcal toxic shock syndrome† or necrotizing fasciitis† (e.g., when the infection is refractory to several hours of aggressive therapy, an undrainable focus is present, or the patient has persistent oliguria with pulmonary edema).<html><head></head><body><sup>105</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>323</sup></body></html>

Immune Globulin Dosage and Administration

Administration

Administer IGIM only by IM injection;<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html> do not administer IV because of risk of serious reactions (e.g., renal dysfunction, acute renal failure, hemolysis, transfusion-related acute lung injury).<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>

Administer IGIV by IV infusion;<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> do not administer IM.<html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> Certain IGIV preparations can be given by IV infusion or sub-Q infusion for treatment of primary humoral immunodeficiency (i.e., Gammagard Liquid 10%,<html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> Gammaked 10%,<html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> Gamunex-C 10%<html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> ); administer all other IGIV preparations only by IV infusion.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html>

Administer immune globulin subcutaneous (Cutaquig 16.5%, Cuvitru 20%, Hizentra 20%, Hyqvia, Xembify 20%) only by sub-Q infusion.<html><head></head><body><sup>294</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>327</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>331</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>340</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>341</sup></body></html>

Administer IGIM by IM injection, preferably into deltoid muscle of upper arm or anterolateral aspect of thigh.<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>

Do not administer routinely into gluteal muscle because of potential for injection-associated injury to the sciatic nerve.<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>

Draw back syringe plunger before IGIM injection to ensure needle is not in a blood vessel.<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>

Prior to administration of IGIM, ensure that patient is not volume depleted and is adequately hydrated.<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>

Do not exceed recommended dosage in patients at increased risk of thrombosis.<html><head></head><body><sup>154</sup></body></html>

Prior to initiation of IGIV infusion, ensure that patients are not volume depleted and are adequately hydrated.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html>

Individualize IV infusion rate based on the specific preparation, indication, tolerability, and individual patient requirements.<html><head></head><body><sup>165</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html>

In general, in patients receiving initial doses of IGIV or switching from one IGIV preparation to another, initiate IGIV using infusion rate at lower end of recommended range and slowly increase to maximum recommended rate only after patient has tolerated several infusions at an intermediate infusion rate.<html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html>

If adverse reactions occur during the IGIV infusion, decrease IV infusion rate or stop the infusion until reactions subside.<html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html>

Use minimum dose and minimum IV infusion rate practicable in patients at risk for renal dysfunction or thrombosis.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>263</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>265</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>266</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>280</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>282</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>292</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>308</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>325</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>326</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>332</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>337</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> <html><head></head><body><sup>339</sup></body></html>

Administer Asceniv 10% only by IV infusion.<html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html>

Do not dilute;<html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> do not mix with other drugs, IV infusion fluids, or other IGIV preparations.<html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html>

Vials are for single use only.<html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html>

If large doses are to be administered, contents of several vials may be pooled into an empty, sterile IV infusion bag using aseptic technique.<html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html>

Administer at room temperature.<html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html>

Primary immunodeficiency: Initiate IV infusions of Asceniv 10% at a rate of 0.5 mg/kg per minute (0.005 mL/kg per minute) for first 15 minutes.<html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> If tolerated, may gradually increase IV infusion rate every 15 minutes up to a maximum of 8 mg/kg per minute (0.08 mL/kg per minute).<html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> If adverse effects related to infusion rate occur, slow or stop the infusion.<html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> If symptoms subside promptly, may resume IV infusion at a lower rate that is comfortable for the patient.<html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html>

Patients at increased risk of thrombosis or renal dysfunction: Use minimum IV infusion rate practicable.<html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html> Consider discontinuing if renal function deteriorates.<html><head></head><body><sup>338</sup></body></html>

Administer Bivigam 10% only by IV infusion.<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html>

Do not dilute;<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> do not mix with other drugs, IV infusion fluids, or other IGIV preparations.<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html>

Vials are for single use only.<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html>

If large doses are to be administered, contents of several vials may be pooled into an empty, sterile IV infusion bag using aseptic technique.<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html>

Discard any partially used vials.<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html>

Administer at room temperature.<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html>

Primary immunodeficiency: Initiate IV infusion of Bivigam 10% at a rate of 0.5 mg/kg per minute (0.005 mL/kg per minute) for first 10 minutes.<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> If tolerated, may gradually increase IV infusion rate every 20 minutes by 0.8 mg/kg per minute to a maximum of 6 mg/kg per minute.<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> If adverse effects related to infusion rate occur, symptoms may disappear if infusion is stopped or slowed.<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> If symptoms subside promptly, may resume IV infusion at a lower rate that is comfortable for the patient.<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html>

Patients at risk for renal dysfunction or thrombosis (including those ≥65 years of age): Use minimum IV infusion rate practicable.<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html> Consider discontinuing if renal function deteriorates.<html><head></head><body><sup>324</sup></body></html>

Administer Carimune NF only by IV infusion.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html>

Administer reconstituted solution through a separate IV line;<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> do not mix with other drugs, IV infusion fluids, or other IGIV preparations.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html>

Administer at room temperature.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html>

Manufacturer states that, although the drug may be filtered, filtering not required.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> If filter is used, those with pore sizes of ≥15 μm are less likely to slow the IV infusion, especially when higher concentrations given;<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> antimicrobial filters (0.2 μm) may be used.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html>

Reconstitute Carimune NF according to the manufacturer’s directions with 0.9% sodium chloride injection, 5% dextrose injection, or sterile water for injection to prepare a solution containing 30, 60, 90, or 120 mg of protein per mL (3, 6, 9, or 12% solution, respectively).<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> Consider patient’s fluid, electrolyte, and caloric requirements when selecting an appropriate diluent and concentration.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html>

After diluent added, swirl vial vigorously to dissolve the drug;<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> to avoid foaming, do not shake.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> Generally dissolves within a few minutes, but may take up to 20 minutes for complete dissolution.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html>

Discard any partially used vials.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html>

If large doses are to be administered, contents of several reconstituted vials of identical concentration and diluent may be pooled into an empty, sterile glass or plastic IV infusion container using aseptic technique.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html>

If reconstituted outside of sterile laminar airflow conditions, promptly administer the reconstituted solution.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> If reconstituted in a sterile laminar flow hood using aseptic technique and reconstituted solution stored under refrigeration, initiate IV infusion within 24 hours after reconstitution.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html>

Primary immunodeficiency in individuals with previously untreated agammaglobulinemia or hypogammaglobulinemia: Administer initial dose of Carimune NF as a 3% solution (30 mg/mL) at an initial IV infusion rate of 0.5 mg/kg per minute.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> After 30 minutes, may increase infusion rate to 1 mg/kg per minute for the next 30 minutes; thereafter, may gradually increase infusion rate in a stepwise manner up to a maximum of 3 mg/kg per minute as tolerated.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> If initial IV infusion well tolerated, higher concentrations may be used for subsequent infusions.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> (See Table 1.) Inflammatory reactions have occurred when initial IV infusion rate >2 mg/kg per minute was used in patients with agammaglobulinemia or hypogammaglobulinemia who had not previously received IGIV or had not received a dose within the last 8 weeks.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> (See Infusion Reactions under Cautions.)

ITP: Administer initial dose of Carimune NF as a 6% solution (60 mg/mL) at an initial IV infusion rate of 0.5 mg/kg per minute.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> After 30 minutes, may increase infusion rate to 1 mg/kg per minute for the next 30 minutes; thereafter, may gradually increase infusion rate in a stepwise manner up to a maximum of 3 mg/kg per minute as tolerated.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html> (See Table 1.)

Patients at risk of developing renal dysfunction (e.g., adults >65 years of age, individuals receiving nephrotoxic drugs, individuals with diabetes mellitus, volume depletion, paraproteinemia, or sepsis): Use IV infusion rate ≤2 mg/kg per minute.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html>

Patients at increased risk of thrombosis (e.g., those with cardiovascular risk factors, advanced age, prolonged periods of immobilization, hypercoagulable disorders, history of venous or arterial thrombosis, use of estrogen-containing preparations, indwelling central vascular catheters, and/or hyperviscosity): Use IV infusion rate ≤2 mg/kg per minute.<html><head></head><body><sup>125</sup></body></html>

Maximum IV infusion rate for patients at risk of renal dysfunction or thrombosis.
